The Ruling on Sujud: Forehead and Nose in the Hanafi School
Explains the order of placing limbs in sujud, the requirement of forehead and nose, and the dispute on sufficing with one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the necessity of the forehead and nose in sujud, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The forehead must touch the ground in sujud; the nose is wajib, but Abu Hanifa allowed sufficing with either.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on what parts of the face are required for a valid prostration. The order of placing limbs is also discussed, but the main focus is whether the nose can substitute for the forehead, and vice versa. We will examine the proofs and the practical consequence of each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is about which parts of the face must touch the ground during sujud: the forehead, the nose, or both? The Hanafi school is divided between Abu Hanifa and his two students (the Sahibayn).
Also consider the opinion of Imam Shafi'i for context.
2
Examine the proof of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn cite the hadith 'I was commanded to prostrate on seven limbs,' which lists the forehead but not the nose. They argue that since the nose is not mentioned, it is like the cheek or chin and not required.
Their position: forehead is fard, nose not required.
3
Examine the proof of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa cites a hadith from Ibn Abbas that includes both the forehead and the nose. He also argues that prostration is achieved by any part of the face, so either suffices, though with karahah.
His position: one may suffice with forehead or nose, but it is makruh to omit the other.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If someone prostrates on the nose only, according to Abu Hanifa the prayer is valid (with karahah); according to the Sahibayn, it is invalid. This difference affects when a person must repeat the prayer.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that of the Sahibayn.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a person who, due to a bandage on the forehead, prostrates only on the nose. According to the mufta bihi, this is invalid unless the forehead touches. However, if the nose only is unavoidable, some later Hanafis allow it with a valid excuse.
Always check the conditions: the hard part of the nose must touch, not the soft part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كبر إلخ: لما روينا أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يكبر عند كل رفع وخفض، وقوله: "ثم وضع يديه"؛ الحديث وائل الله أنه قال: رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد وضع ركبتيه قبل يديه، وإذا نهض رفع يديه قبل ركبتيه، رواه أبو داود وهو حجة على مالك حيث يقول: إن شاء وضع يديه أولاً ثم ركبتيه، وإن شاء عكس رمز الحقائق: ۰۸/۱، ۵۹] ووضع الوجه بين الكفين؛ لما روي: "أن النبي علي كان إذا سجد وضع يديه حذاء أذنيه . (فتح مسكين بعكس النهوض: أي القيام يعني في القيام يرفع وجهه أولا ثم يده ثم ركبتيه وهذا عكس ما فعل جميعاً أولاً، حاصله أن كل ما كان أقرب من الأرض يوضع على الأرض أولاً، ويرفع عن الأرض آخراً. (فتح مستخلص) بأنفه أي ما صلب منه؛ إذ أنهم أجمعوا على عدم جوازه بما لان منه. (فتح) وجبهته لأن النبي عليه واظب عليه وقال الشافعي: السجود بهذه الكيفية فرض؛ لقوله علي: "لا يقبل الله صلاة من لا يمس جبهته على الأرض، وهو عندنا محمول على نفي الكمال والتهديد ووضع جميع أطراف الجبهة ليس بشرط بالإجماع، فإذا اقتصر على بعض الجبهة جاز وإن قل، لكن ينبغي وضع أكثرها للمواظبة، وضم ما صلب من الأنف مع الجبهة في السجود واجب. (فتح، مستخلص) بأحدهما: أي الأنف والجبهة وهذه إشارة إلى جواز الاكتفاء بأحدهما أيضاً مع الكراهة عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: إن سجد على الجبهة دون الأنف جاز، وبالعكس لا، فالسجود على الجبهة فرض عندهما؛ لقوله عليه: "أمرت أن أسجد على سبعة أعضاء، وعد منها الجبهة، ولو كان الأنف محل السجود لذكره، فصار كالخد والذقن، وله: روي عن ابن عباس لما أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : أمرت أن أسجد على سبع، ولا أكف الشعر ولا الثياب، الجبهة والأنف واليدين والركبتين والقدمين" الحديث رواه مسلم رمز الحقائق ٥٩/١] ولأن السجود يتحقق -
Then he said takbir etc.: Because we narrated that the Prophet used to say takbir at every lowering and raising, and his statement: 'Then he placed his hands' – the hadith of Wa'il that he said: I saw the Messenger of Allah when he prostrated, he placed his knees before his hands, and when he rose, he raised his hands before his knees. Narrated by Abu Dawud, and it is a proof against Malik where he says: If he wishes he places his hands first then his knees, and if he wishes he reverses. [Ramooz al-Haqa'iq: 59/1, 59] And he placed his face between his palms, because it is narrated that the Prophet when he prostrated placed his hands level with his ears. (Fath, Miskeen) 'Opposite to rising': i.e., standing means in standing he raises his face first, then his hands, then his knees, and this is the opposite of what he did all first. The conclusion is that everything closer to the ground is placed on the ground first and raised from the ground last. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'With his nose': i.e., the hard part of it, for they have agreed on the impermissibility of prostrating on the soft part. (Fath) 'And his forehead': because the Prophet adhered to it. And al-Shafi'i said: Prostration in this manner is fard, due to his statement: 'Allah does not accept the prayer of one who does not place his forehead on the ground.' And it is interpreted by us as negation of perfection and threat. And placing all parts of the forehead is not a condition by consensus. So if one limits to some of the forehead, it is permissible even if little, but one should place most of it due to constancy. And including the hard part of the nose with the forehead in prostration is wajib. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'With one of them': i.e., the nose and forehead, and this indicates the permissibility of sufficing with one of them as well, with karahah, according to Abu Hanifah. And the two said: If he prostrates on the forehead without the nose, it is permissible, and vice versa not, so prostrating on the forehead is fard according to them, due to his statement: 'I was commanded to prostrate on seven limbs' and he counted the forehead from them. If the nose were a place of prostration, he would have mentioned it, so it becomes like the cheek and chin. And for him (Abu Hanifah): It is narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah said: 'I was commanded to prostrate on seven, not to gather the hair or the garment: the forehead and the nose, the hands, the knees, and the feet' – the hadith narrated by Muslim. [Ramooz al-Haqa'iq: 59/1] And because prostration is achieved...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the rulings of sujud. It begins by stating that the Prophet said takbir for every movement in prayer, specifically when going into sujud. The order of placing limbs is taught by the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr: knees first, then hands, then face. This is a proof against Malik who allows different orders. The opposite order is for rising. The ruling on the organs of prostration: the hard part of the nose and the forehead must touch the ground. Abu Hanifa holds that sufficing with either one is permissible, though disliked, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the forehead is obligatory (fard) and the nose is not sufficient alone. The proof for the Sahibayn is the hadith of the seven limbs where the forehead is mentioned but the nose is not explicitly listed; thus the nose is like the cheek. However, Abu Hanifa cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas where both forehead and nose are mentioned. The text also notes that Shafi'i considers the forehead touching the ground as fard, but the Hanafi school interprets the hadith as a negation of perfection. The authority weight: the ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi as well; the fuqaha later favored the position of the Sahibayn that the forehead is fard.

Key Terms
سجود (sujud): Prostration, a pillar of prayer, requiring specific limbs to touch the ground.
جبهة (jabhah): The forehead; the part of the face that must touch the ground in sujud.
أنف (anf): The nose; the hard part (bone) must touch the ground in sujud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person prostrates on the nose only (hard part) without the forehead touching?
A) Valid but disliked (makruh).
B) Invalid; the prayer is not valid.
C) Valid only if the forehead is injured.
D) Invalid only if done intentionally.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the forehead must touch the ground in sujud (fard according to the mufta bihi), and the hard part of the nose is also wajib, but relying on the nose alone is invalid unless following the weaker opinion of Abu Hanifa with a valid excuse.

Tasmi' and Tahmid: Division of Dhikr Between Imam and Follower
Explains distribution of tasmi' and tahmid between imam and follower, with proof and intra/inter-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the division of tasmi' and tahmid between the imam and follower in congregational prayer, identify the primary hadith proof, and explain the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah; follower says Rabbana wa laka al-hamd — the hadith divides, not shares.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common practical question in congregational prayer: Who says which dhikr when rising from ruku'? The Hanafi school provides a clear division based on the Prophet's instruction. You will learn the precise rulings for the imam, the follower, and the individual, along with the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage specifies the dhikr for the imam, the follower (mu'tam), and the one praying alone (munfarid). Write down what each says.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited in the passage: 'When the imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, then say Rabbana wa laka al-hamd.' Understand how this divides the dhikr.
The Arabic word is 'qasama' (he divided).
3
Analyze the linguistic and legal implication
The division (qasam) negates partnership (shirkat). Explain why the imam does not say tahmid aloud, and the follower does not say tasmi'.
This is the core Hanafi argument.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the imam says tahmid silently. Why? Because the Prophet combined both dhikrs and to avoid self-forgetfulness.
The relied-upon position is the Zahir al-Riwayah (imam says only tasmi' aloud).
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that both imam and follower say both dhikrs, based on the principle of following the imam. Evaluate the Hanafi response.
The practical difference (thamarah) is what the follower says in Hanafi vs Shafi'i prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واكتفى الإمام بالتسميع، والمؤتم بالتحميد والمنفرد بهما. بالتسميع: أي التسميع فقط؛ لقوله عليه: "إذا قال الإمام: سمع الله لمن حمده، فقولوا: ربنا ولك الحمد" قسم بينهما، وهي تنافي الشركة، فلا يقول الإمام ربنا ولك الحمد وقالا : يقوله الإمام سراً؛ لأنه عليه كان يجمع بينهما؛ ولأنه حرض غيره فلا ينسى نفسه، وقال الشافعي: يأتي الإمام والمأموم بالذكرين؛ لأن المؤتم يتابع الإمام فيما يفعل. وسمع بمعنى قبل، يقال: سمع الأمير كلام فلان أي قبله وهو دعاء لقبول الحمد، واللام في "لمن" للمنفعة، والهاء في "حمده" للسكنة والاستراحة، وقيل: هاء الضمير. (فتح) بالتحميد: أي يكتفي المؤتم بالتحميد فقط. وصيغ التحميد أربع ربنا لك الحمد، وربنا ولك الحمد، واللهم ربنا لك الحمد، واللهم ربنا ولك الحمد، وكل من . هذه الأربع منقول عنه عليه، لكن "اللهم ربنا ولك الحمد" أحسن وأفضل، وقيل: اللهم ربنا لك الحمد" بدون الواو، واختلفوا في الواو، فقيل زائدة، وقيل: عاطفة، تقديره: ربنا حمدناك ولك الحمد، وقال الشافعي: يأتي المؤتم بالتسميع أيضاً. والمنفرد بهما: أي يأتي المنفرد بالتسبيح والتحميد، وهو الأصح. (طائي)
And the imam is content with tasmi', and the follower with tahmid, and the one praying alone with both. Tam'i' means only tasmi'; due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'When the imam says "Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah", then say "Rabbana wa laka al-hamd"'; he divided between them, and it negates sharing, so the imam does not say "Rabbana wa laka al-hamd". And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the imam says it silently; because he (peace be upon him) used to combine between them; and because he urges others so he does not forget himself. And al-Shafi'i said: The imam and follower both say both remembrances; because the follower follows the imam in what he does. And sami'a means accepted; it is said: 'the amir heard the speech of so-and-so' meaning he accepted it; and it is a prayer for acceptance of praise. And the lam in 'liman' is for benefit, and the ha in 'hamdihi' is for calmness and rest, and it is said: it is the pronoun ha. Tahmid: meaning the follower is content with tahmid only. And the forms of tahmid are four: 'Rabbana laka al-hamd', 'Rabbana wa laka al-hamd', 'Allahumma Rabbana laka al-hamd', 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd'. All of these are transmitted from him (peace be upon him), but 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' is better and more virtuous. And it is said: 'Allahumma Rabbana laka al-hamd' without the waw. And they differed regarding the waw; it is said it is extra, and it is said it is conjunctive, its meaning: 'Rabbana hamidnaka wa laka al-hamd'. And al-Shafi'i said: the follower also says tasmi'. And the one praying alone with both: meaning he comes with tasbih and tahmid, and that is the most correct.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering the distribution of the tasmi' (saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah') and tahmid (saying 'Rabbana lakal hamd') between the imam and the follower in congregational prayer. The base ruling is that the imam is content with only tasmi', the follower with only tahmid, and the person praying alone says both. The primary proof is the hadith: 'When the imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, then say Rabbana wa laka al-hamd.' This divides the dhikr, preventing the imam from also saying tahmid aloud. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the imam should say tahmid silently, because the Prophet combined both and to encourage others without neglecting himself. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that both imam and follower say both dhikrs, arguing that the follower follows the imam in all actions. The Hanafi response is that the hadith explicitly divides, negating participation. The passage also explains the linguistic meaning of sami'a (to accept) and the forms of tahmid, noting that 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' is the most virtuous. For the individual praying alone, they say both tasmi' and tahmid, which is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
التسميع (al-tasmi'): The imam's utterance of 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' upon rising from ruku'.
التحميد (al-tahmid): The follower's utterance of 'Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' or variants.
المنفرد (al-munfarid): The one praying alone, not in congregation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the follower say when the imam says 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah'?
A) Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah
B) Rabbana wa laka al-hamd
C) Both A and B
D) Something else

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi prayer, the imam is responsible for tasmi', the follower for tahmid, and the individual says both — based on the explicit division in the hadith.

Rulings of Prostration in Hanafi Fiqh
Details the rules and proofs for sujud including limbs and women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on prostration (sujud) and distinguish between obligatory, sunnah, and disliked elements, applying the proofs from hadith.

Core Idea
Direct forehead-to-earth contact is not obligatory; a barrier is permitted in necessity, but placing the toes is fard.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the rules of prostration (sujud) in prayer, including the permissibility of barriers, the recommended postures for men, and the distinct posture for women. The Hanafi school relies on specific hadith proofs to derive these rulings, which sometimes differ from other madhahib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling categories
Sujud has obligatory (fard), sunnah, and disliked (makruh) elements. The text specifies which actions fall under each category.
For example, placing the tips of the feet is fard; exposing the armpits is sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites ahadith: hadith of Anas for permissibility of a barrier, hadith of Ibn Umar for exposing armpits, hadith of Maimunah for separating belly from thighs.
These are ahadith ahad with zanni thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does each hadith prove the ruling? The hadith of Anas shows that the command to 'place the forehead' is satisfied even with a garment barrier, so direct earth contact is not a condition.
The Prophet's action in the hadith demonstrates the permissibility.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Al-Shafi'i considers a barrier (like turban folds) impermissible; Imam Malik considers tasbih obligatory. The Hanafi school disagrees based on ahadith.
These differences affect the ruling's practical application.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that using a barrier is makruh but valid, and the recommended postures (idba', mujafah, etc.) are sunnah muakkadah. The toes must be placed on the ground (fard).
This is the position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كيفية الصلاة
أو بكور عمامته [هو دورها إن وجد حجم الأرض أو على فاضل ثوبه أي فإنه يكره أيضاً، وكذا فاضل ثوبه، والباء بمعنى "على"، وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز بكور العمامة؛ لقوله عليه: "مكن جبهتك وأنفك من الأرض"، ولنا: حديث أنس قال: "كنا نصلي مع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في شدة الحر، فإذا لم يستطع أحدنا أن يمكن جبهته من بسط ثوبه، فيسجد عليه"، وورد أنه صلى في ثوب واحد متوشحاً به يتقي بفضوله حر الأرض وبردها، وما رواه لا ينافي ما قلنا؛ لأن التمكين يوجد معه؛ إذ لا يشترط مماسة الأرض بهما إجماعاً. ولو سجد على كفه وهي على الأرض جاز على الأصح وعلى فخذه من غير عذر لا يجوز، وعلى ركبتيه لا يجوز مطلقاً، ولو سجد على ظهر من في صلاته يجوز، وعلى ظهر من يصلي صلاة غيره، أو ليس في الصلاة لا يجوز، وإن سجد على شيء لا يدرك حجمه لا يجوز كالقطن المحلوج والثلج والتبن ونحو ذلك. (عيني، فتح) وأبدى ضبعيه [ أي أظهر عضديه في غير زحمة وهو من الإبداء. (طائي، عيني)] لقوله عليه لابن عمر " وأبد ضبعيك عن جنبيك". والضبع بسكون الموحدة وسط العضد أي يباعدهما عن جنبيه. (عيني، مستخلص) وجافي بطنه إلخ لحديث ميمونة: "أنه عليه كان إذا سجد جافى بين يديه حتى أن بهمة لو أرادت أن تمر بين يديه مرت". تبيين الحقائق: ٣٠٦/١] والبهمة بفتح الموحدة وسكون الهاء: الأنثى من صغار ولد الشاة، والحكمة في المجافاة إظهار كل عضو بنفسه، وأنه غير معتمد على غيره في أداء الخدمة، وقيل: إن كان الصف لا يجافي حذرا من إضرار الجار. (عيني، فتح ووجه إلخ لقوله عليه: "إذا سجد العبد يسجد كل عضو منه، فليوجه من أعضائه إلى القبلة ما استطاع". وصرح في "التجنيس" بكراهة تركه كما يكره لو وضع قدماً ورفع أخرى بلا عذر، ووضع رؤوس القدمين حالة السجود فرض حتى لو سجد ورفع أصابع رجليه من الأرض لا يجوز. (فتح، مستخلص) ثلاثا: أي يقول: "سبحان الأعلى" ثلاث مرات، وذلك أدناه، وعن مالك: التسبيح فيه فرض. [رمز الحقائق: ٥٩/١] والمرأة إلخ والأمة فيه كالحرة، وتنخفض أي تضم نفسها، فلا تبدي ضبعيها وتلزق بالزاء والصاد من باب علم أي تصل بطنها بفخذيها؛ لأنه عليه مر على امرأتين تصليان فقال: "إذا سجدتما ضما بعض اللحم إلى بعض"، والمرأة تخالف الرجل في خصال ترفع يديها إلى منكبيها، وتضع يمينها على شمالها تحت ثدييها، ولا تجافي بطنها عن فخذيها، وتضع يديها على فخذيها بحيث تبلغ رؤوس أصابعها ركبتيها ولا تبدي إبطيها في السجود، وتجلس متوركة في التشهد ولا تفرج أصابعها في الركوع ، ولا تؤم الرجال وتكره جماعتهن، ويقف الإمام وسطهن، ولا تنصب أصابع القدمين في السجود، ولا يستحب في حقها الجهر بالقراءة في الجهرية. (فتح)
Chapter on the manner of prayer: Or with the turban (its folds if there is the thickness of the ground or on the excess of his garment, for that is also disliked, likewise the excess of his garment, and the ba is in the meaning of 'on'. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible with the folds of the turban because of his statement: 'Place your forehead and nose on the ground.' And we have the hadith of Anas: 'We prayed with the Prophet (peace be upon him) in severe heat, and if one of us could not place his forehead, he would spread out his garment and prostrate on it.' And it is reported that he prayed in one garment, wrapping it around him, using its excess to protect from the heat and cold of the ground. What he narrated does not contradict what we said because placing is achieved; touching the ground with both is not a condition by consensus. If he prostrates on his palm while it is on the ground, it is permissible according to the most correct opinion; on his thigh without an excuse, not permissible; on his knees, not permissible at all. If he prostrates on the back of someone who is in his prayer, it is permissible; on the back of someone praying a prayer other than his, or not in prayer, not permissible. If he prostrates on something whose thickness is not perceived, it is not permissible, like combed cotton, snow, straw, and the like. 'And he exposes his armpits' means he shows his upper arms when not in a crowd, based on his statement to Ibn Umar: 'Expose your armpits from your sides.' The word 'dhab' with a silent b means the middle of the upper arm, i.e., he distances them from his sides. 'And he separates his belly from his thighs' according to the hadith of Maimunah: 'When he prostrated, he would separate his hands so much that if a young goat wanted to pass between them, it would pass.' The wisdom in separation is to show each limb independently and that it is not relying on another in performing service. It is said: if the row is narrow, he does not separate for fear of harming the neighbor. 'And he directs his toes toward the qibla' according to his statement: 'When the servant prostrates, every limb prostrates, so let him direct as many of his limbs toward the qibla as he can.' It is explicitly stated in al-Tajnisa that leaving it is disliked, just as it is disliked to put one foot and raise the other without excuse. Placing the tips of the feet during prostration is obligatory, so if he prostrates and raises his toes from the ground, it is not permissible. 'Three times' means he says 'Subhana al-A'la' three times, and that is the minimum. From Malik, the tasbih is obligatory. 'And the woman' etc., and the female slave is like a free woman. She lowers herself, i.e., compresses herself, so she does not expose her armpits, and she attaches her belly to her thighs; because he passed by two women praying and said: 'When you prostrate, press some of the flesh to some.' The woman differs from the man in several traits: she raises her hands to her shoulders, places her right hand on her left under her breasts, does not separate her belly from her thighs, places her hands on her thighs so that her fingers reach her knees, does not expose her armpits in prostration, sits mutawarrikan in tashahhud, does not spread her fingers in ruku, does not lead men, and her congregation is disliked; the imam stands in their middle; she does not extend the toes of her feet in prostration, and raising the voice in recitation is not recommended for her in prayers where recitation is loud.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof on the manner of prostration (sujud) in prayer, from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by addressing the rule regarding prostrating on the folds of the turban or on excess garment: according to the Hanafi school, this is disliked (makruh), whereas al-Shafi'i considered it impermissible. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Anas where the Prophet allowed prostrating on a garment in extreme heat, indicating that direct contact with the ground is not a condition. The text then covers the ruling of prostrating on the palm, thigh, or knee: the correct opinion permits the palm if it is on the ground, but not the thigh without excuse or the knee absolutely. Prostrating on the back of someone in the same prayer is permissible, but on one praying a different prayer or not praying is not. Prostrating on something that does not have a palpable thickness (like cotton) is invalid. The sunnah acts of prostration are then explained: exposing the armpits (idba'), separating the belly from the thighs (tajafiyah), directing the toes toward the qibla, and saying the tasbih three times (minimum). The text includes a brief intra-madhhab variation on the obligation of tasbih (from Malik). Finally, the passage details the specific rules for women in prostration: they are to compress themselves, not separate, and place hands on thighs—contrary to men—and various other differences in prayer postures. The authority of these rulings is from Zahir al-Riwayah, supported by ahadith and consensus.

Key Terms
بكور عمامته (bukūr ʿimāmatihī): The folds of the turban when piled up, creating a thickness.
أبدى ضبعيه (abdā ḍabʿayhi): He exposes his armpits; i.e., raises his arms away from his sides.
مجافاة (mujāfāh): Separating the belly from the thighs during prostration.
تسبيح (tasbīḥ): Saying 'Subhana al-A'la' (Glory to the Most High) in sujud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a man prostrating while keeping his toes off the ground?
A) It is permissible if he has an excuse.
B) It is not permissible because placing the tips of the feet is obligatory.
C) It is sunnah to keep them on the ground but not obligatory.
D) It is disliked (makruh) but the prostration is valid.

Key Takeaway
Prostration in Hanafi fiqh requires placing the toes firmly on the ground (fard), while the recommended postures (exposing armpits, separating belly) are sunnah muakkadah, and a barrier may be used when needed but is disliked.

Rising from Sujud, Sitting, and Second Prostration
Rulings on sujud and transitions with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on raising the head from sujud, the obligation of tranquility in pillars, and the ruling on leaning on hands when standing, and explain the proofs and the differences with Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Tranquility in every pillar is wajib; leaning on hands when standing is makruh tanzihi, not sunnah.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its sharh details the actions after the first prostration: how to raise the head, the obligation of tranquility, and the disputed ruling on using hands to stand. It also covers the second prostration and the transition to the second rak'ah. Understanding these rulings requires distinguishing between wajib and sunnah elements and recognizing the effect of illa in the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Pinpoint each action (raising head, second sujud, leaning, thana, etc.) and state the hukm level (wajib, sunnah, makruh, etc.) according to the Hanafi school.
The passage distinguishes between wajib (itmi'nan) and sunnah (leaning for elderly).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: tawatur for two prostrations, hadith of Abu Huraira for standing on balls of feet, and reasoning for qiyas on leaning.
Tawatur is qat'i al-thubut; the hadith is ahad but used as counter-evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling. For itmi'nan: it completes the rukn. For leaning: the absence of takbir and the companion's apology show it is not legislated.
The 'illa for tranquility is tashbih al-rukniya (completing the pillar).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference on raising amount (Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad ibn Salama) and the inter-madhhab dispute on leaning (Shafi'i vs. Hanafi).
The mu'tamad is Abu Hanifa's view; Shafi'i's view is interpreted due to old age.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine why the mufta bihi position is preferred: Abu Hanifa's raising view is more cautious; leaning is makruh based on stronger evidence (Ibn Umar's apology, lack of takbir).
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani and the later Hanafi masters.

Source Text

أن الاطمئنان في الأركان واجب؛ لأنه شرع لتكميل ركن مقصود، بخلاف القومة بعد رفع الرأس من الركوع والجلسة بين السجدتين؛ لأنهما شرعتا للفرق بين الركنين، وتكرار السجدة ثبت بفعل الرسول المنقول عنه تواترا، وأما وجه تكراره فقيل: إنه تعبد لا يطلب فيه المعنى كأعداد الركعات، وقيل: إن الشيطان أمر بسجدة فلم يفعل فسجد سجدتين ترغيما له وقيل: الأولى إشارة إلى أنا خلقنا من الأرض، والثانية إلى أنا نعود إليها قال تعالى: ﴿ مِنْهَا خَلَقْنَاكُمْ وَفِيهَا نُعِيدُكُمْ (طه:٥٥) وقيل: الأولى لشكر الإيمان، والثانية لبقائه. (فتح) بلا اعتماد إلخ: فإن اعتمد وقعد كره تنزيهاً، وقال الشافعي: يجلس جلسة خفيفة، ثم ينهض معتمداً بيديه عليها؛ لما فعله عليه، وهو محمول عندنا على حالة الكبر؛ ولهذا فعله ابن عمر ثم اعتذر فقال: إن رجلي لا تحملاني، ولو كانت مشروعة لشرع التكبير عند الانتقال منها إلى القيام، وأيضاً روى أبو هريرة: أنه عليه كان ينهض على صدور قدميه (فتح عيني وقعود: أي بلا قعود عند رفع الرأس من الثانية إلى القيام. (طائي) كالأولى: أي يفعل فيها مثل ما فعل في الأولى. (طائى) لا يثني ولا يتعوّذ: أي لا يأتي بالثناء، وهو سبحانك اللهم إلخ، ولا التعوذ؛ لأنهما لم يشرعا إلا في أول الصلاة. [ رمز الحقائق: ٦٠/١] ولا يرفع يديه إلخ: أي على السنة، وجه أي لا يسن الرفع مؤكداً إلا في هذه المواضع، ولا يرد عليه الرفع في الدعاء والاستسقاء؛ لأن الرفع فيهما مستحب، وعلى تقدير تسليم كون الرفع فيهما من السنن يجاب بأنه من الزوائد والحصر باعتبار السنن الأصلية. (فتح)
Then he raises his head [i.e., the worshiper after finishing the first prostration]. There is a difference in the amount of raising: it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that if it is closer to sitting, it is permissible; if closer to the ground, it is not permissible. Muhammad ibn Salama said: If he raises his head such that it is not ambiguous to the observer that he has raised, it suffices. It is said: If his forehead leaves the ground such that wind flows between his forehead and the ground, then he returns it, it suffices for the two prostrations. The correct and relied-upon is the first. He prostrates with tranquility. Know that tranquility (itmi'nan) in the pillars is obligatory because it was legislated to complete a intended pillar, unlike the standing after raising the head from ruku and the sitting between the two prostrations; they were legislated to distinguish between the two pillars. The repetition of the prostration is established by the action of the Messenger transmitted by tawatur. As for the wisdom of its repetition, it is said: It is devotional, not requiring meaning, like the numbers of rak'ahs; it is said: Satan was commanded one prostration but did not do it, so he prostrated twice to enrage him; it is said: The first indicates that we were created from earth, the second that we return to it; Allah says: 'From it We created you, and into it We shall return you' (Taha 55); it is said: The first is thanks for faith, the second for its preservation. 'Without leaning' etc.: If he leans and sits, it is disliked (makruh tanzihan). Al-Shafi'i said: He sits lightly, then stands up leaning on his hands, based on his action; but in our school it is interpreted as due to old age; for this reason Ibn Umar did it and then apologized, saying: 'My legs do not carry me.' If it were legislated, the takbir would be legislated when moving from it to standing. Also, Abu Huraira reported that he used to rise on the balls of his feet. 'And sitting': i.e., without sitting when raising the head from the second [prostration] to standing. 'Like the first': i.e., he does in it the same as he did in the first. 'He does not recite thana nor ta'awwudh': i.e., he does not recite the praise (Subhanaka Allahumma...) nor the seeking of refuge, because they are only legislated at the beginning of the prayer. 'And he does not raise his hands except in seven places': i.e., according to the sunnah, meaning raising is not strongly sunnah except in these places, and the raising in supplication and prayer for rain does not contradict it, because raising there is mustahabb; and assuming it is sunnah, it is from the extras and the restriction is to the original sunan.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof detailing the actions after the first prostration: raising the head, the second prostration, and the transition to the second rak'ah. The text begins by discussing the amount of raising from sujud: the relied-upon Hanafi position (from Abu Hanifa) is that one must raise enough to be closer to a sitting posture; if closer to the ground, it is invalid. Other opinions from Muhammad ibn Salama and a weak view are mentioned but the first is mu'tamad. Then the ruling on tranquility (itmi'nan) in all pillars is stated as obligatory (wajib), distinguishing it from the standing after ruku and the sitting between prostrations which are for separation and not obligatory in the same way. The repetition of the prostration is confirmed by tawatur, and various wisdoms are given. The Hanafi-Sha'fi dispute on leaning on hands when standing up: Shafi'i permits it based on a prophetic action, but Hanafi interprets it as due to old age, supported by Ibn Umar's apology and the fact that takbir is not prescribed for that movement. The Hanafi ruling is that it is makruh tanzihi to lean on hands when standing. Then the rules for the second rak'ah are summarized: it is like the first except that thana and ta'awwudh are not repeated (only at the beginning of prayer), and the hands are not raised except in the seven specific places enumerated later. The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah with clear dalil from hadith and qiyas.

Key Terms
الاطمئنان (al-iṭmi'nān): Tranquility: pausing briefly in each pillar of prayer until the joints settle.
الاعتماد (al-iʿtimād): Leaning on the hands when standing up from prostration.
الثناء (al-thanā'): The opening praise (Subhanaka Allahumma) recited at the beginning of prayer.
التعوذ (al-taʿawwudh): Saying 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan' before recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on leaning on one's hands when standing up from prostration?
A) It is sunnah muakkadah.
B) It is permissible but makruh tanzihi, and only allowed for the elderly or those with an excuse.
C) It is wajib for everyone.
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
Tranquility in every pillar is wajib, and leaning on hands when standing is makruh tanzihi, not sunnah, in the Hanafi school.

The Seven Places Where Hands Are Raised: The Mnemonic FQ'SSM'J
Mnemonic list of seven places for raising hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the seven places where raising the hands is sunnah muakkadah according to the Hanafi school and explain the mnemonic 'faqʿaṣ ṣamaʿj'.

Core Idea
Raising hands is sunnah muakkadah only at seven specific places in worship, not at every transition.

This lesson presents a concise mnemonic known as 'f-q-'-s-ṣ-m-'-j' that encapsulates the seven occasions where raising the hands is a strongly recommended sunnah (sunnah muakkadah) in the Hanafi school. Understanding this list prevents the common mistake of raising hands during other actions like ruku or rising. We'll decode each letter and see how the eight letters actually represent seven places.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the mnemonic letters
Learn the string 'فقعس صمعج' (fa', qaf, 'ayn, sad, mim, 'ayn, jim). These are the initial letters of the places.
The mnemonic is unique to the Hanafi tradition and aids in recall.
2
Match each letter to a place
Associate each letter with the corresponding worship action: F=opening takbir, Q=qunut, 'A=two 'Eids, S=istilam (touching Black Stone), Ṣ=Safa, M=Marwah, 'A='Arafah and Muzdalifah, J=first and middle Jamarat.
Note: the second 'A represents both 'Arafah and Muzdalifah together.
3
Resolve the apparent eight into seven
Observe that Ṣ (Safa) and M (Marwah) are counted as one place (both part of sa'y), so the eight letters correspond to seven distinct occasions.
The text explicitly states that Safa and Marwah are treated as one to match the hadith count.
4
Apply the rule in practice
When performing salat, hajj, or umrah, raise hands only at these moments; do not raise at ruku, sujood, or other standing positions.
Raising hands elsewhere is makruh tanzih (disliked) or bid'ah if done consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقعس صمعج [ أي سبعة مواضع يعبر عنها بحروف فقعس صمعج: فالفاء تكبيرة الافتتاح، والقاف القنوت، والعين العيدان، والسين استلام الحجر الأسود، والصاد الصفا والميم المروة، والعين الثاني عرفة الجمع وهو المزدلفة، والجيم الجمرة الأولى والوسطي.] أي إلا في سبع مواطن، ولما كان فيه تطويل ضبط المصنف بهذه الحروف، فإن قيل: في الحديث سبع مواطن وهذه الحروف ثمانية دالة على ثمانية مواضع فكيف التطبيق؟ فالجواب أن الصفا والمروة كالواحد، ولهذا صارت سبعة، وقد نظمها الشاعر في قوله: ارفع يديك لدى التكبير مفتتحاً وقانتا وبه العيدان قد وصفا وفي الوقوفين ثم الجمرتين معا وفي استلام كذا في مروة وصفا
Fa', Qaf, 'Ayn, Sin, Sad, Mim, 'Ayn, Jim (mnemonic for seven places): i.e., seven places expressed by the letters FQ'SSM'J. The fa' is the opening takbir; the qaf is qunut; the 'ayn is the two 'Eids; the sin is the touching of the Black Stone; the sad is al-Safa and the mim is al-Marwah; the second 'ayn is 'Arafah and al-Jam' which is Muzdalifah; the jim is the first and middle Jamarah. I.e., except in seven places, and because it was lengthy, the author summarized with these letters. If it is said: In the hadith there are seven places, but these letters are eight indicating eight places; how is the correspondence? The answer is that al-Safa and al-Marwah are counted as one, thus becoming seven. And the poet has versified it: 'Raise your hands at the opening takbir, in qunut, and at the two 'Eids as described, at the two standings (at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah), then the two Jamarat together, at the touching (of the Black Stone), likewise at al-Marwah as described.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a mnemonic summary (matn) of the seven places where raising the hands is strongly sunnah (muakkadah) during worship. The author uses the letters 'f-q-'-s-ṣ-m-'-j' to correspond to: 1) Takbirat al-ihram (opening takbir), 2) Qunut (in witr prayer), 3) The two 'Eid prayers, 4) Istilam al-hajar al-aswad (touching the Black Stone during tawaf), 5) Al-Safa (during sa'y), 6) Al-Marwah (during sa'y), 7) 'Arafah (standing at Arafat), 8) Muzdalifah (standing at Muzdalifah, combined with 'Arafah as one), and the two Jamarat (stoning). The text resolves the apparent eight letters by noting that al-Safa and al-Marwah count as one place (both are part of sa'y). A poetic mnemonic is also provided. The authority of these rulings is that raising hands in these places is sunnah muakkadah; in other places (like ruku, rising, etc.), it is not sunnah according to the Hanafi school. The passage is a memorization anchor for students.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (rafʿ al-yadayn): Raising the hands (to the ears/shoulders) as a sunnah action in specific places.
فقعس صمعج (faqʿaṣ ṣamaʿj): Mnemonic acronym for the seven places: F=opening takbir, Q=quanut, 'A=two 'Eids, S=istilam, Ṣ=Safa, M=Marwah, 'A= 'Arafah/Muzdalifah, J=Jamarat.
قنوت (qunūt): Supplication in witr prayer; raising hands is sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Raising the hands is a sunnah muakkadah only in seven specific places: the opening takbir, qunut, the two 'Eids, istilam, Safa and Marwah, 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, and the Jamarat.

The First Sitting (Qa'dah) and Its Ruling
Hanafi matn describing sitting posture and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the first sitting in prayer, describe its prescribed posture, and distinguish between wajib and fard in this context.

Core Idea
The first sitting is wajib, not fard—its omission requires compensation, not invalidation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq describes the precise posture for the first sitting after the second sajdah. It also establishes that reciting the tashahhud at this point is wajib. Understanding why this sitting is wajib rather than fard is essential for correct prayer and for knowing what to do if it is forgotten.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the first sitting (qa'dah) after the second sajdah of the second rak'ah. The text states it is wajib after the first two rak'ahs.
The word 'وجوباً' (obligatorily) indicates wujub, not fard.
2
Learn the prescribed posture
Memorize the six elements: (1) spread left foot, (2) sit on it, (3) erect right foot, (4) direct fingers toward qiblah, (5) place hands on thighs, (6) spread fingers. This posture is called tawarruk.
The text says 'وهي تتورّك' (and this is tawarruk). Note: In Hanafi fiqh, tawarruk is for women; men sit on the left foot with right foot erect.
3
Identify the recitation in this sitting
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is recited here. The Hanafi school adopts this specific wording.
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud begins with 'al-tahiyyatu lillah...'.
4
Distinguish first sitting from last sitting
The first sitting is wajib; the last sitting is fard. Wajib in Hanafi means obligatory but established by zanni (speculative) evidence; omission requires sajdah al-sahw. Fard is established by qat'i (certain) evidence; omission invalidates the prayer.
This distinction is not in the matn but is in the commentary; it is crucial for understanding the ruling.
5
Apply the ruling: consequence of forgetting
If the first sitting is omitted, the prayer is still valid but sajdah al-sahw is wajib. If the last sitting is omitted, the prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
This step synthesizes from the ruling; it prepares for practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا فرغ من سجدتي الركعة الثانية افترش رجله اليسرى، وجلس عليها ونصب يمناه، ووجه أصابعه نحو القبلة، ووضع يديه على فخذيه، وبسط أصابعه، وهي تتورّك، وقرأ تشهد ابن مسعود نه، وفيما بعد الأوليين وجوباً.
When he finishes the two prostrations of the second rak‘ah, he spreads his left foot, sits on it, erects his right foot, directs his fingers towards the qiblah, places his hands on his thighs, spreads his fingers—and this is tawarruk—and recites the tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud, may Allah be pleased with him, and after the first two [rak‘ahs of the obligatory prayer] it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn describing the posture for the first sitting (qa‘dah) after the second sajdah. The description includes spreading the left foot and sitting on it, erecting the right foot, facing the fingers toward the qiblah, placing the hands on the thighs with fingers spread. This posture is called tawarruk. The text also states that reciting the tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud after the first two rak‘ahs of the obligatory prayer is wajib (obligatory). According to the Hanafi school, the first sitting is wajib, while the last sitting is fard (obligatory at a higher level). This distinction is explained in the commentary: the last qa‘dah is fard, the first is wajib.

Key Terms
القعدة الأولى (al-qa‘dah al-ūlā): The sitting after the second sajdah of the second rak‘ah in a prayer of three or four rak‘ahs.
تتورّك (tawarruk): A sitting posture where the left foot is spread and the right foot is erected, sitting on the left foot.
تشهد ابن مسعود (tashahhud Ibn Mas‘ūd): The specific wording of the tashahhud transmitted from Ibn Mas‘ud, beginning with 'al-tahiyyatu lillah...'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The first sitting in prayer is wajib; its prescribed posture includes spreading the left foot, sitting on it, erecting the right, and reciting the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud.

The Dispute on Raising Hands at Ruku'
Hanafi response to Shafi'i on raising hands at ruku'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on raising hands during prayer and evaluate the evidence against the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Raising hands only at the opening takbir is the Hanafi sunnah; all other raises are makruh.

This passage addresses a well-known inter-madhhab dispute regarding raising the hands (raf‘ al-yadayn) during prayer. While the Shafi‘i school considers it sunnah to raise hands at ruku‘ and when rising, the Hanafi school restricts it to the opening takbir. The passage presents two key proofs from the Companions Ibn Mas‘ud and Jabir, and an argument of abrogation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its hukm level
Identify the Hanafi ruling: raising hands is only at the opening takbir; raising at other points is makruh tahrimi. Note that makruh tahrimi is a disliked action that is close to haram but does not invalidate the prayer.
The hukm level comes from the hanafi_position_box.
2
Identify the Shafi‘i position and its proof
The Shafi‘i school holds that raising hands at ruku‘ and rising is sunnah. Their evidence (not fully quoted here) includes hadiths of Ibn ‘Umar.
Understand the opposing view to appreciate the dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi primary proofs
Examine the hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud: he states that the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and ‘Umar did not raise hands except at the opening takbir. Also the hadith of Jabir where the Prophet criticizes raising hands during prayer. How do these prove the Hanafi view?
Focus on the explicit negation in Ibn Mas‘ud's hadith and the prohibition in Jabir's hadith.
4
Evaluate the abrogation argument
The Hanafi response addresses any authentic reports that the Prophet himself raised hands: such practice was later abrogated by the consistent practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, as mentioned in Sharh al-Majma‘.
This is a key element of the Hanafi tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرفع في غير هذه المواضع مكروه، وقال الشافعي: يرفع يديه أيضاً عند الركوع وعند الرفع منه، ولنا: قول ابن مسعود : صليت مع رسول الله له هو أبي بكر وعمر فلم يرفعوا أيديهم إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة، وعن جابر قال: خرج عليه علينا وقال: "مالي أراكم رافعي أيديكم كأنها أذناب خيل شمس اسكنوا في الصلاة" ولئن سلمنا وقوع الرفع . عليه عند الركوع والرفع منه فنقول: إنه منسوخ، كما في شرح المجمع.
And raising [hands] in other than these places is makruh. Al-Shafi‘i said: He raises his hands also at the time of ruku‘ and at the time of rising from it. Our proof: the statement of Ibn Mas‘ud: ‘I prayed with the Messenger of Allah, with Abu Bakr and ‘Umar, and they did not raise their hands except at the opening of the prayer.’ And from Jabir: He came out to us and said: ‘Why do I see you raising your hands as if they are the tails of shy horses? Be still in prayer.’ And even if we concede the occurrence of raising (according to him) at ruku‘ and rising, we say: it is abrogated, as in Sharh al-Majma‘.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute about raising the hands (raf‘ al-yadayn) during prayer. The Hanafi position is that raising hands is only done at the opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram). Raising at ruku‘, rising from ruku‘, or other positions is makruh (disliked). Imam al-Shafi‘i holds that raising hands is also performed at the time of ruku‘ and when rising from ruku‘. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud stating that the Prophet, Abu Bakr, and ‘Umar only raised their hands at the opening of the prayer. Another proof is the hadith of Jabir where the Prophet prohibited raising hands during prayer, comparing it to the tails of shy horses. If it is argued that the Prophet did raise hands at ruku‘, the Hanafi response is that such practice was later abrogated, as mentioned in Sharh al-Majma‘. Note: The phrase 'shams' means 'shy' or 'restive'.

Key Terms
الرفع (al-raf‘): Raising the hands during prayer, specifically at certain postures.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; an action that is not sinful but is discouraged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which part(s) of the prayer are hands raised?
A) Only at the opening takbir
B) At the opening takbir and before ruku‘
C) At every movement
D) At the opening takbir and after rising from ruku‘

Key Takeaway
Raising hands during prayer is a disputed sunnah; the Hanafi school restricts it to the opening takbir based on the practice of the Companions and the principle of abrogation.

The Preponderance of Ibn Mas'ud's Tashahhud
Argument for preferring Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the tashahhud wording and list the ten preponderance (tarjīḥ) reasons given by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud is preponderant due to ten proofs: authenticity, widespread practice, and precise wording.

In this lesson, we examine the famous dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the exact wording of the tashahhud. The Hanafi school relies on the transmission of Ibn Mas'ud, and the passage we study provides ten compelling reasons for its preference. Understanding this dispute illustrates how Hanafi scholars apply the method of tarjīḥ when hadiths appear to conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing tashahhud versions
Read the Arabic text and note the exact wording of Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud (with 'wa' and definite articles) and the wording attributed to Ibn 'Abbas (without 'wa' and with 'salamun').
The difference is subtle but significant for the school's practice.
2
Recognize the Hanafi claim of preponderance
The text asserts that Ibn Mas'ud's version is well-known in the six authentic books and that Ibn 'Abbas's version is not found in any reliable collection.
This sets the stage for the ten reasons.
3
Work through the ten reasons systematically
List each reason: lack of confusion, inclusion of 'wa', definiteness of 'al-salam', Abu Bakr's teaching, widespread practice, ‘Ali's command, the physical gesture, authenticity, freedom from weakness, and strictness on precise wording.
These are both textual and practical proofs.
4
Categorize the types of evidence
Group the reasons: textual (authenticity, chains), linguistic (conjunctions, definiteness), practical (companion practice, teaching, command), and authority (strictness of Ibn Mas'ud).
Tarjīḥ considers multiple dimensions.
5
Appreciate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference results in Hanafis reciting 'wa al-salawatu wa al-tayyibatu' and 'al-salamu 'alayka', while Shafi'is omit the 'wa' and use 'salamun'.
Each school teaches its version as sunnah mu'akkadah or wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشهد ابن مسعود له: هو أن يقول: "التحيات الله والصلوات والطيبات السلام عليك أيها النبي ورحمة الله وبركاته، السلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين، أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً عبده ورسوله". وقال الشافعي: تشهد بتشهد ابن عباس ما، وهو أن يقول: "التحيات المباركات الصلوات الطيبات الله، سلام عليك ركاته، سلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين، أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً رسول الله". والمعروف في الكتب الستة تشهد ابن مسعود ، و لم يخرج تشهد ابن عباس أحد ممن التزم الصحة، وكل من رواه يرويه على خلاف قول الشافعي مع ضعف كل رواياته وتشهد ابن مسعود له يترجح عليه بوجوه: الأول: أنه لا اضطراب فيه الثاني: اشتماله على واو العطف، فكان ثناء متعدداً، الثالث: تعريف السلام المقتضي للاستغراق الرابع: تعليم الصديق تشهد ابن مسعود على المنبر كتعليم القرآن الخامس: عمل أهل العلم والنقل به ولم يعمل بتشهد ابن عباس غير الشافعي ،وأتباعه السادس: أمره علي ابن مسعود له أن يعلمه الناس، والأمر للوجوب فلا ينزل عن الاستحباب السابع: أخذه علي بكف ابن مسعود بين كفيه حين . ففيه زيادة اهتمام واستثبات الثامن كونه مرويا في الصحاح التاسع كون رواياته خالية عن ا الضعف العاشر : تشديد عبد الله على أصحابه حين أخذ عليهم الواو في "والصلوات" والألف في "السلام عليك".
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud: it is that he says: 'al-tahiyyatu lillah, wa al-salawatu wa al-tayyibatu, al-salamu ‘alayka ayyuha al-nabiyyu wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuhu, al-salamu ‘alayna wa ‘ala ‘ibadillah al-salihin, ashhadu alla ilaha illallah, wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan ‘abduhu wa rasuluhu.' Al-Shafi‘i said: tashahhud with the tashahhud of Ibn ‘Abbas, and it is that he says: 'al-tahiyyatu al-mubarakatu al-salawatu al-tayyibatu lillah, salamun ‘alayka rahmatuhu [etc.], salamun ‘alayna wa ‘ala ‘ibadillah al-salihin, ashhadu alla ilaha illallah, wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan rasulullah.' And the well-known in the six books is the tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud, and no one who adhered to authenticity narrated the tashahhud of Ibn ‘Abbas. And everyone who narrated it narrated it contrary to al-Shafi‘i’s statement, with weakness in all his narrations. And the tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud is preponderant over it in several ways: First: it has no confusion. Second: it includes the conjunction 'waw', so it is multiple praise. Third: the definite article in 'al-salam' indicates generality. Fourth: Abu Bakr taught the tashahhud of Ibn Mas‘ud from the pulpit like teaching the Qur’an. Fifth: the people of knowledge and transmission acted upon it, and no one acted upon the tashahhud of Ibn ‘Abbas except al-Shafi‘i and his followers. Sixth: ‘Ali ordered Ibn Mas‘ud to teach it to the people, and a command indicates obligation, so it does not fall below recommendation. Seventh: ‘Ali took the hands of Ibn Mas‘ud in his own hands, and in this is increased care and confirmation. Eighth: it is narrated in the authentic collections. Ninth: its narrations are free from weakness. Tenth: ‘Abdullah insisted on his companions the 'waw' in 'wa al-salawat' and the 'alif' in 'al-salamu ‘alayka'.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the exact wording of the tashahhud recited in the sitting. The Hanafi school adopts the version transmitted from Ibn Mas‘ud, while Imam al-Shafi‘i adopts the version of Ibn ‘Abbas. The passage lists ten reasons why Ibn Mas‘ud’s version is preponderant (tarjih). The first is that Ibn Mas‘ud’s version has no internal discrepancy. The second is that it includes the conjunction 'wa', which multiplies the praise (i.e., 'al-tahiyyatu lillah wa al-salawatu wa al-tayyibatu' as opposed to 'al-tahiyyatu al-mubarakatu al-salawatu al-tayyibatu' without the conjunction). The third is that 'al-salam' is definite, implying complete and comprehensive peace. The fourth is that Abu Bakr taught this tashahhud from the pulpit, elevating it to the status of Qur’anic instruction. The fifth is the widespread practice of the scholars. The sixth is the command of ‘Ali to Ibn Mas‘ud to teach it, and a command implies at least recommendation. The seventh is the physical gesture of ‘Ali showing extra care. The eighth is that it is found in the six authentic hadith collections. The ninth is that its chains are free from weakness. The tenth is that Ibn Mas‘ud was strict about the exact wording, indicating its precision. These arguments establish the Hanafi preference.

Key Terms
تشهد ابن مسعود (tashahhud Ibn Mas‘ūd): The specific wording of the tashahhud as transmitted from Ibn Mas‘ud, including the conjunctions and specifics.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Preponderance; giving weight to one evidence over another when they conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It includes the conjunction 'waw'

C) It is free from weakness
D) It is shorter and more concise

Key Takeaway
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is preponderant over that of Ibn 'Abbas due to ten proofs encompassing authenticity, practice, and precision, establishing the Hanafi recitation.

The Second Sitting: Recitations and Rulings
Rulings for the second sitting: tashahhud, salat, and du'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sending blessings upon the Prophet in the second sitting and identify the difference with other schools.

Core Idea
Sending blessings upon the Prophet in the second sitting is sunnah, not fard.

This lesson covers the recitations of the second sitting (qa'dah) in prayer according to the Hanafi school. You already know that the sitting itself is fard. Here we focus on what is recited: tashahhud, sending blessings upon the Prophet, and du'a. The key question is whether these are obligatory or recommended. Hanafi fiqh considers the salat upon the Prophet to be sunnah, while the Shafi'i school holds it as fard. This distinction has practical implications for the validity of your prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet Muhammad during the second sitting. According to the Hanafi school, the hukm is sunnah.
Sunnah means recommended; omitting it does not invalidate the prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
Hanafi scholars rely on the absence of an explicit command in the Quran or mutawatir hadith that makes salat upon the Prophet obligatory in prayer. The imperatives in the Quran to send salat are general and not specifically tied to the prayer's second sitting.
The dalil is not explicitly quoted in this passage, but it is derived from usul principles.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school considers salat upon the Prophet in the tashahhud as fard, citing a hadith where the Prophet said, 'Do not send blessings upon me in a maimed manner' and other evidences.
Other schools also differ; Hanbali and Maliki have their own positions.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa) and supported by the principle that acts of worship are not deemed obligatory without clear, decisive evidence. The absence of such evidence keeps the ruling at sunnah.
Al-Hidayah and other authoritative texts uphold this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اكتفى بالفاتحة. والقعود الثاني كالأول، وتشهد وصلى على النبي عليك ودعا بما يُشبه القرآن بعد الصلاة على النبي علمه،
He sufficed with al-Fatiha. And the second sitting is like the first, and he recites the tashahhud, and sends blessings upon the Prophet, and supplicates with what resembles the Qur’an after the salat upon the Prophet.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about the second sitting (qa‘dah) in prayer. The text says that the second sitting is like the first in posture. The recitations in this sitting include: (1) the tashahhud, (2) sending blessings upon the Prophet (salat ‘ala al-nabi), and (3) making dua using supplications that resemble Qur’anic verses. The commentary clarifies that the salat upon the Prophet is sunnah according to the Hanafi school, while the Shafi‘i school considers it fard. The du'a should be similar to Qur’anic phrases such as 'Rabbana la tu’akhidhna...' or 'Rabbana atina fi al-dunya hasanah...' The phrase 'sufficed with al-Fatiha' means that in the first two rak‘ahs, one may only recite al-Fatiha without an additional surah, although it is better to add a surah.

Key Terms
القعود الثاني (al-qa‘dah al-thāniyah): The final sitting in a prayer of four rak‘ahs, after the fourth sajdah.
صلى على النبي (ṣallā ‘alā al-nabī): Sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad (salat and salam) after the tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mustahab

Key Takeaway
The second sitting in prayer includes tashahhud (wajib), salat upon the Prophet (sunnah), and du'a (sunnah); omitting the sunnah elements does not invalidate the prayer.

The Second Sitting: Hanafi Iftirash vs Tawarruk
Compares the second sitting to the first in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the second sitting posture, contrast it with the positions of al-Shafi'i and Malik, and evaluate the Hanafi dalil from the hadith.

Core Idea
The second sitting in prayer follows the first: iftirash, not tawarruk, based on a prophetic prohibition.

In the Hanafi school, the second sitting (qa'dah) of prayer mirrors the first: one sits on the left foot with the right foot erect (iftirash). This differs from the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, which prescribe tawarruk in the second sitting or both. The dispute hinges on a hadith that the Prophet forbade iq'a' and tawarruk. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: the second sitting is like the first, meaning iftirash (sitting on the left foot, right foot erect). This is a sunnah mu'akkadah.
The posture is not fard; sitting itself is wajib, but the specific manner is sunnah.
2
Identify opposing views and their proofs
Al-Shafi'i holds tawarruk in the second sitting only; Malik holds tawarruk in both. Their proofs include practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and hadith reports.
Tawarruk: sitting on the left buttock with feet protruding to the right.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school cites a hadith that the Prophet forbade tawarruk and iq'a' in prayer. This is a direct prohibition, indicating that tawarruk is not recommended.
The hadith is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) of ahad status.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi worshipper sits with iftirash in both sittings; a Shafi'i uses iftirash in the first and tawarruk in the second; a Maliki uses tawarruk in both.
This affects the validity of prayer? No, posture is sunnah, not fard. But following the school's preferred posture avoids makruh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقعود الثاني كالأول: [ أي كالقعود الأول عندنا خلافاً للشافعي ومالك] يعني أنه كما يفترش رجله اليسرى، ويجلس عليها، وينصب يمناه في القعدة الأولى فكذا في الثانية، وقال مالك: يتورك فيهما، وقال الشافعي يتورك في الثانية، وقد روي أنه عليه السلام نهى عن الإقعاء والتورك في الصلاة، وهو حجة عليهما (فتح)
And the second sitting is like the first: [That is, like the first sitting according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i and Malik] meaning that just as he spreads his left foot, sits on it, and erects his right in the first sitting, so too in the second. And Malik said: He sits mutawarrikan in both. And al-Shafi'i said: He sits mutawarrikan in the second. And it has been narrated that he (peace be upon him) forbade iq'a' and tawarruk in prayer, and this is proof against them. (Fath)
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage discusses the posture of the second sitting (qa'dah) in prayer. The Hanafi position is that the second sitting is identical to the first: one sits on the left foot with the right foot erect. Both Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i differ: Malik prescribes tawarruk (sitting on the left buttock with feet protruding) in both sittings, while al-Shafi'i prescribes tawarruk only in the second. The Hanafi proof is a hadith that the Prophet forbade both iq'a' (squatting) and tawarruk in prayer. This hadith is used as evidence against the opposing views.

Key Terms
التورك (al-tawarruk): A sitting posture where one sits on the left buttock, with both feet protruding to the right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect (iftirash).
D) Any posture is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school insists on iftirash (sitting on left foot) in both sittings, rejecting tawarruk due to the prophetic prohibition.

Tashahhud and Salawat: Hanafi Positions and Proofs
Ruling on tashahhud and salawat in second sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tashahhud and salawat in the second sitting, list the ten proofs for the preponderance of Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud, and explain why salawat is sunnah not fard in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defends Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud with ten proofs and holds salawat to be sunnah, contrary to the Shafi'i position of fard.

The second sitting of prayer is the locus of two important recitations: the tashahhud (testimony of faith) and the salawat (blessings on the Prophet). The Hanafi school has distinct positions on both, each backed by strong evidence. This lesson unpacks the ten proofs for the preferred tashahhud and the reasoning behind the sunnah status of salawat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Determine what the passage rules upon: tashahhud (wajib, specific wording) and salawat (sunnah) in the second sitting.
2
Analyze the ten proofs for tashahhud
List each of the ten proofs for the preponderance of Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud over Ibn Abbas's version, and assess how each proof strengthens the Hanafi position.
The proofs are numbered in the text; trace each one carefully.
3
Understand the shahada story
Read the narrative from the Isra' that explains the origin of the tashahhud wording.
This story shows the heavenly basis for the recitation.
4
Examine the dispute on salawat
Identify the differing views: Hanafi says sunnah, Shafi'i says fard. Note the objection about the comparison to Ibrahim and the Hanafi response.
The text provides a specific answer using the Qur'anic analogy.
5
Conclude the practical difference
State the consequence of omitting each: omission of tashahhud requires sajdah al-sahw; omission of salawat does not require compensation.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome of the dispute).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل من رواه يرويه على خلاف قول الشافعي مع ضعف كل رواياته وتشهد ابن مسعود له يترجح عليه بوجوه: الأول: أنه لا اضطراب فيه الثاني: اشتماله على واو العطف، فكان ثناء متعدداً، الثالث: تعريف السلام المقتضي للاستغراق الرابع: تعليم الصديق تشهد ابن مسعود على المنبر كتعليم القرآن الخامس: عمل أهل العلم والنقل به ولم يعمل بتشهد ابن عباس غير الشافعي ،وأتباعه السادس: أمره علي ابن مسعود له أن يعلمه الناس، والأمر للوجوب فلا ينزل عن الاستحباب السابع: أخذه علي بكف ابن مسعود بين كفيه حين . ففيه زيادة اهتمام واستثبات الثامن كونه مرويا في الصحاح التاسع كون رواياته خالية عن ا الضعف العاشر : تشديد عبد الله على أصحابه حين أخذ عليهم الواو في "والصلوات" والألف في "السلام عليك". و"التحيات" العبادات القولية والصلوات" العبادات البدنية، و"الطيبات" العبادات المالية أي كلها الله لا لغيره، قيل: إن جبريل عليه أمر النبي ل ليلة الإسراء أن يحيي ربه بهذه التحية، فحى ربه بها فأكرمه الله تعالى بثلاث مقابلة الأولى: السلام عليك أيها النبي، والثاني: رحمة الله والثالث: وبر كاته، زيادة الخيرات، فأحب عليه يعني هذه الكرامة لأمته فقال: السلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين فأجاب جبريل وقال: أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأشهد أن محمداً عبده ورسوله. (فتح ملخصا) وتشهد أي في القعدة الثانية. (طائي) وصلى فيها بأن يقول: "اللهم صل على محمد" إلخ "وبارك على محمد" إلخ، وهي سنة عندنا، وعند الثلاثة فرض أي في القعدة الثانية بعد التشهد، فإن قيل: إن المشبه به يكون أعلى فيكون الصلاة على إبراهيم أعلى الصلاة على النبي لوله فالجواب : أنه لا يلزم أن يكون المشبه به أعلى أو مساويا، بل قد يكون أدنى كقوله تعالى: مَثَلُ نُورِهِ كَمِشْكَاةٍ فِيهَا مِصْبَاحٌ ﴾ (النور:٣٥) وسبب وقوعه كون المشبه به مشهوراً، فهو من باب إلحاق غير المشهور بالمشهور لا الناقص بالكامل، وههنا أجوبة أخرى أيضاً تركناها مخافة التطويل. واعلم أن الصلاة عليه أمر بها في السنة الثانية من الهجرة، وقيل: في ليلة الإسراء، وهي سنة عندنا، وعند الشافعي فرض. (فتح)
And everyone who narrated it narrates it contrary to the statement of al-Shafi'i, despite the weakness of all his narrations. And the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) is preponderant over it in ten ways: First: it has no inconsistency. Second: it includes the conjunction 'waw', so it is multiple praise. Third: the definiteness of 'al-salam' necessitates comprehensiveness. Fourth: the teaching of al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr) of Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud on the minbar is like teaching the Quran. Fifth: the people of knowledge and transmission acted upon it and no one acted upon Ibn 'Abbas's tashahhud except al-Shafi'i and his followers. Sixth: 'Ali ordered Ibn Mas'ud to teach it to the people, and a command indicates obligation, so it is not less than recommendation. Seventh: 'Ali took Ibn Mas'ud's hand in his hands at the time, which shows extra care and certitude. Eighth: it is narrated in the authentic collections. Ninth: its narrations are free from weakness. Tenth: 'Abdullah (Ibn Mas'ud) stressed to his companions when he took from them the waw in 'wa al-salawat' and the alif in 'al-salamu 'alayka'. And 'al-tahiyyat' are verbal acts of worship, 'al-salawat' are physical acts of worship, and 'al-tayyibat' are financial acts of worship, meaning all are for Allah, not for other than Him. It is said that Jibril (peace be upon him) commanded the Prophet (peace be upon him) on the night of Isra' to greet his Lord with this greeting, so he greeted his Lord with it, and Allah honored him with three in return: first: 'al-salamu 'alayka ayyuha al-nabiyyu', second: 'rahmatullahi', third: 'wa barakatuhu', increase of good things. So he (peace be upon him) loved this honor for his nation and said: 'al-salamu 'alayna wa 'ala 'ibadillahi al-salihin'. Then Jibril responded and said: 'Ashhadu an la ilaha illa Allah wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan 'abduhu wa rasuluhu.' (Fath summarized) And tashahhud i.e. in the second sitting. (Ta'i) And he sends salawat in it by saying: 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' etc. and 'barik 'ala Muhammad' etc., which is sunnah according to us, and according to the three (other imams) it is fard i.e. in the second sitting after tashahhud. If it is said: The object of comparison is higher, so the salawat on Ibrahim would be higher than the salawat on the Prophet, so the answer: It is not necessary that the object of comparison be higher or equal; rather it may be lower, as in Allah's saying: 'The likeness of His light is as a niche wherein is a lamp' (al-Nur 35). And the reason for it is that the object of comparison is well-known, so it is a case of likening the unknown to the known, not the deficient to the perfect. And there are other answers which we have left out to avoid prolixity. And know that sending salawat upon him was commanded in the second year of the Hijra, some say on the night of Isra', and it is sunnah according to us, and according to al-Shafi'i it is fard. (Fath)
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings: tashahhud and salawat. Regarding tashahhud, the Hanafi school follows the version of Ibn Mas'ud. The author presents ten proofs from various angles (textual consistency, Sunnah, practice of companions, etc.) to establish its superiority over other versions, particularly over the version of Ibn 'Abbas used by al-Shafi'i. Regarding salawat (sending blessings on the Prophet), the Hanafi position is that it is sunnah in the second sitting, not fard as al-Shafi'i holds. The objection that the comparison in the salawat formula (as you sent blessings on Ibrahim) implies Ibrahim is higher is answered with a Qur'anic example and common usage.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith (al-tahiyyat lillah...) in the sitting of prayer.
الصلاة على النبي (al-salāt 'alā al-nabī): Sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) in the tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Fard, invalidating prayer if omitted.

D) Makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adheres to Ibn Mas'ud's tashahhud as wajib based on ten proofs and considers salawat in the second sitting as sunnah, not fard, contrary to the Shafi'i school.

Permissible Du'a in the Second Sitting
Specifies the type of du'a permissible after salawat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given du'a is permissible or prohibited in the second sitting of prayer according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
In prayer, du'a must be from the Quran or Sunnah in wording; everyday speech is prohibited.

The second sitting (qa'dah akhirah) is a moment for tashahhud, salawat, and du'a. The Hanafi school teaches that the du'a should be in the words of the Quran or the Prophet ﷺ, not in everyday human speech. This discipline preserves the reverence of prayer and distinguishes it from worldly conversation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the du'a wording
Look at the du'a text. Is it a direct quotation from the Quran or a well-known sunnah du'a?
E.g., 'Rabbana atina fid-dunya hasanah' is Quranic.
2
Check if it resembles human speech
If the du'a is not Quranic/sunnah, examine whether it could naturally be said to another human. For example, 'Give me a car' is a request that can be made to people.
If yes, it is prohibited (kalam al-nas).
3
Apply the ruling
If Quranic/sunnah → permissible and recommended. If kalam al-nas → prohibited and invalidates the prayer if uttered deliberately.
4
Evaluate borderline cases
Some du'as may use Quranic phrases slightly modified. If the meaning remains reverent and not mundane, it may be allowed. However, strict adherence to Quranic/sunnah wording is safer.
Hanafi school prefers verbatim Quran or authentic hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما يشبه القرآن: أي بالأدعية الموجودة في القرآن، مثل: ﴿رَبَّنَا لا تُؤَاخِذْنَا ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٨٦)، أو ﴿رَبَّنَا آتِنَا فِي الدُّنْيَا حَسَنَةٌ وَفِي الْآخِرَةِ حَسَنَةٌ )) (البقرة :(۲۰۱)، أو ورَبَّنَا اغْفِرْ لِي وَلِوَالِدَيَّ (إبراهيم: ٤١) (فتح) من  كتاب الصلاة ك فصل في كيفية الصلاة أو السنة لا كلام الناس: أي لا يدعو بما يشبه كلامهم، وهو ما لا يستحيل سؤاله من العباد، نحو: أعطني كذا، وزوجني امرأة رمز الحقائق: ٦١/١]
With that which resembles the Quran: i.e., from the supplications found in the Quran, such as: 'Our Lord, do not impose blame upon us' (al-Baqarah 286), or 'Our Lord, give us in this world good and in the Hereafter good' (al-Baqarah 201), or 'Our Lord, forgive me and my parents' (Ibrahim 41) (Fath). Or the Sunnah, not the speech of people: i.e., he should not supplicate with that which resembles the speech of people, which is what it is not impossible to ask from created beings, such as: 'Give me such and such' and 'Marry me to a woman' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:61).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage specifies what du'a is permissible after salawat in the second sitting. The matn teaches that one should make du'a with what resembles the Quran or Sunnah. The commentary clarifies: permissible du'as are those found in the Quran, like the verses quoted, or authentic sunnah supplications. It forbids du'as that resemble everyday speech, such as direct requests for worldly matters that could be asked of people (e.g., 'give me this' or 'marry me to a woman'). The reason is that such requests degrade the reverence of prayer.

Key Terms
ما يشبه القرآن (mā yushbihu al-qur'ān): Supplications that are either verbatim from the Quran or similar in style and content.
لا كلام الناس (lā kalām al-nās): Supplications that resemble everyday human speech, which are prohibited in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'O Allah, give me a car.'
B) 'Our Lord, grant us good in this world and good in the Hereafter.'
C) 'O Allah, marry me to a beautiful woman.'
D) 'O Allah, make me rich.'

Key Takeaway
In the second sitting, du'a must be from the Quran or Sunnah; words that could be said to people are forbidden.
